  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_ffa347c4-7938-400c-9bb1-3e2fa124bea5'
      });
    }
    
    var initializeSiwa = function() {
      var APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL =  "https://appleid.cdn-apple.com/appleauth/static/jsapi/appleid/1/en_US/appleid.auth.js"
      loadScript(APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL, initAppleId);

        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/42390708?book_show_action=true">Jan 08, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer42390708" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18380210546903418497">One of the most brilliant pieces of translation I've ever come across. You can hardly believe that all these wonderful jokes and word-games weren't originally composed in English. I wish I knew some Polish, so that I could compare with the original.<br/><br/>The most impressive sequences, which have been widely quoted, come from the story where one of the inventors builds a machine that can write a poem to any specification, no matter how bizarre. "A poem about love, treachery, indomitable courage, on the</span>
  <span id="freeText18380210546903418497" style="display:none">One of the most brilliant pieces of translation I've ever come across. You can hardly believe that all these wonderful jokes and word-games weren't originally composed in English. I wish I knew some Polish, so that I could compare with the original.<br /><br />The most impressive sequences, which have been widely quoted, come from the story where one of the inventors builds a machine that can write a poem to any specification, no matter how bizarre. "A poem about love, treachery, indomitable courage, on the subject of a haircut, and every word to start with the letter S!" says his friend. And within a few seconds, the machine has produced:<br /><br /><i>Seduced, shaggy Samson snored.<br />She scissored short. Sorely shorn,<br />Soon shackled slave, Samson sighed,<br />Silently scheming,<br />Sightlessly seeking<br />Some savage, spectacular suicide.</i><br /><br />The love poem where all the metaphors come from the language of mathematics is nearly as good.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="18380210546903418497" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780156235501" href="/book/show/1118485.The_Cyberiad">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">everyone</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/63444-seth?shelf=sf-f-h">sf-f-h</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/63444-seth?shelf=science-fiction">science-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/63444-seth?shelf=masterpieces">masterpieces</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer4681171" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7043787295318009128">If you're only going to read one Lem in your life...<br/>...seek medical help. There are several <i>essential</i> Lem books and stories.<br/><br/>And this is one of them. Both of them. Something like that. It's an essential Lem book of essential Lem stories.<br/><br/>The basic outline is simple: two robot inventors (they are robots and they invent robots... whether they invented themselves is indeed an open question) appear, one or the other or both, in some fashion, in a series of stories set in a universe of robots. The inve</span>
  <span id="freeText7043787295318009128" style="display:none">If you're only going to read one Lem in your life...<br />...seek medical help. There are several <i>essential</i> Lem books and stories.<br /><br />And this is one of them. Both of them. Something like that. It's an essential Lem book of essential Lem stories.<br /><br />The basic outline is simple: two robot inventors (they are robots and they invent robots... whether they invented themselves is indeed an open question) appear, one or the other or both, in some fashion, in a series of stories set in a universe of robots. The inventors--friends, rivals, and each the only one capable of understanding the other's genius--are Nasrudin-like figures, both wise and fools, both creating problems and solving them, meeting common (robot) folk and uncommon (robot) world leaders. <br /><br />They try to one-up one another, they try to help one another, and through it all they teach by doing and do by teaching. Maybe the comparison to Mullah Nasrudin is more apt than I'd realized. <br /><br />If Mullah Nasrudin were two space-travelling robot inventors....<br /><br />Yeah, that's the book.<br /><br />Read it.<br /><br />And btw, it's hilarious, it's a quick read, and it's really easy to get ahold of.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7043787295318009128" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_2635584092" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2635584092" />
    <a title="Vit Babenco" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/19283284-vit-babenco"><img alt="Vit Babenco" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1365604577p2/19283284.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2635584092?book_show_action=true">Dec 24, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Vit Babenco" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Vit Babenco" href="/user/show/19283284-vit-babenco">Vit Babenco</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780156235501" href="/book/show/1118485.The_Cyberiad">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2635584092" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13652984531537441390">It all happened in days of yore, long before the invasion of cyberpunk… Cyborgs were merry and mischievous then… And they were cunningly inventive…<br/><blockquote>Next there was a boom, a puff of yellow smoke, and something came rocketing out, a form as blurry as a tornado and with the general consistency of a sandstorm; it arced through the air so fast that no one really got a good look at it anyway. Whatever it was flew a hundred paces or more and landed without a sound; the curtain that had been wrapped arou</blockquote></span>
  <span id="freeText13652984531537441390" style="display:none">It all happened in days of yore, long before the invasion of cyberpunk… Cyborgs were merry and mischievous then… And they were cunningly inventive…<br /><blockquote>Next there was a boom, a puff of yellow smoke, and something came rocketing out, a form as blurry as a tornado and with the general consistency of a sandstorm; it arced through the air so fast that no one really got a good look at it anyway. Whatever it was flew a hundred paces or more and landed without a sound; the curtain that had been wrapped around it floated to the earth, glass bells tinkling oddly in that perfect silence, and lay there like a crushed strawberry. Now everyone could see the beast clearly – though it wasn't clear at all, but looked a little like a hill, rather large, fairly long, its color much like its surroundings, a clump of dried-up weeds. The King's huntsmen unleashed the whole pack of automated hounds (mainly Saint Cybernards and Cyberman pinschers, with an occasional high-frequency terrier); these hurled themselves, howling and slavering, at the crouching beast. The beast didn't rear back, didn't roar, didn't even breathe fire, but only opened its two eyes wide and reduced half the pack to ashes in a trice.</blockquote><br />This is an absolute beast created by the unsurpassed robotic constructors: optimistic and resilient Trurl and his pessimistic and ironic colleague Klapaucius. Their colourful adventures and mishaps throughout the entire universe are simply unrepeatable…<br /><blockquote>Everyone knows that dragons don't exist. But while this simplistic formulation may satisfy the layman, it does not suffice for the scientific mind. The School of Higher Neantical Nillity is in fact wholly unconcerned with what does exist. Indeed, the banality of existence has been so amply demonstrated, there is no need for us to discuss it any further here. The brilliant Cerebron, attacking the problem analytically, discovered three distinct kinds of dragon: the mythical, the chimerical, and the purely hypothetical. They were all, one might say, nonexistent, but each non-existed in an entirely different way.</blockquote><br />As one can clearly see all the reflections in the book are profoundly philosophic and strictly scientific…<br /><blockquote>Pugg meanwhile sat propped up against the barrel and read, as that diamond pen which the Demon employed to record everything it learned from the oscillating atoms squeaked on and on, and he read about how exactly Harlebardonian wrigglers wriggle, and that the daughter of King Petrolius of Labondia is named Humpinella, and what Frederick the Second, one of the paleface kings, had for lunch before he declared war against the Gwendoliths, and how many electron shells an atom of thermionolium would have, if such an element existed, and what is the cloacal diameter of a small bird called the tufted twit, which is painted by the Wabian Marchpanes on their sacrificial urns, and also of the tripartite taste of the oceanic ooze on Polypelagid Diaphana, and of the flower Dybbulyk, that beats the Lower Malfundican hunters black and blue whenever they waken it at dawn, and how to obtain the angle of the base of an irregular icosahedron, and who was the jeweler of Gufus, the left-handed butcher of the Bovants, and the number of volumes on philately to be published in the year seventy thousand on Marinautica, and where to find the tomb of Cybrinda the Red-toed, who was nailed to her bed by a certain Clamonder in a drunken fit…</blockquote><br />It sounds so familiar… It looks like <b>Stanisław Lem</b> could predict the appearance of the Internet so many years ago.<br />Do androids dream of electric fairytale tellers?</span>
  <a data-text-id="13652984531537441390" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_586606512" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/586606512" />
    <a title="Bradley" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4213258-bradley"><img alt="Bradley" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1508811508p2/4213258.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/586606512?book_show_action=true">Jul 20, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Bradley" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Bradley" href="/user/show/4213258-bradley">Bradley</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4213258-bradley?shelf=2019-shelf">2019-shelf</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4213258-bradley?shelf=sci-fi">sci-fi</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4213258-bradley?shelf=humor">humor</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer586606512" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer125287286208873381">While I was initially tempted to treat this collection of 1965 short SF stories with kid gloves because I was already a huge fan of Solaris, I didn't quite understand that this collection was already a heavyweight of humor, satire, and delight. <br/><br/>Where the hell have I been? I should have read this back when I was a kid! Alongside Hitchhiker's Guide! As I read this, I gave a constant chuckle-rumble, especially with the Seven Sallies of Trurl and Klapaucius. These two master-builder robots get along</span>
  <span id="freeText125287286208873381" style="display:none">While I was initially tempted to treat this collection of 1965 short SF stories with kid gloves because I was already a huge fan of Solaris, I didn't quite understand that this collection was already a heavyweight of humor, satire, and delight. <br /><br />Where the hell have I been? I should have read this back when I was a kid! Alongside Hitchhiker's Guide! As I read this, I gave a constant chuckle-rumble, especially with the Seven Sallies of Trurl and Klapaucius. These two master-builder robots get along with their wits and near-infinite capability to make things. Anything. And they are tricksters. Very funny tricksters.<br /><br />The one time that Trurl made a poetry machine, I was f***ing spoiled by some of the best math poetry I've ever read, and here's the kicker: This was translated from Polish. Hell, it was translated into several dozen languages. But the English translation retained ALL its flavor. :) It was honestly funny. <br /><br />All of this was light, clever, and always to the point. These are traditional fables, almost like the old Chivalric tradition, but add the element of gods granting everyone's wishes to the downfall of the wisher, and you've got a very good idea about what's going on here. Oh, and almost every character is a robot. The wisecracking kind.<br /><br />I admit I've read a number of things *like* this, but never to this one's high quality. This is a perfect cure for grimdark malaise. :)</span>
  <a data-text-id="125287286208873381" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2936956235" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

    <a title="MJ Nicholls" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2386804-mj-nicholls"><img alt="MJ Nicholls" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1374341850p2/2386804.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2936956235?book_show_action=true">Aug 16, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="MJ Nicholls" class="user" itemprop="url" name="MJ Nicholls" href="/user/show/2386804-mj-nicholls">MJ Nicholls</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780141394596" href="/book/show/21079821-the-cyberiad">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2386804-mj-nicholls?shelf=penguin-classics">penguin-classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2386804-mj-nicholls?shelf=science-fiction">science-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2386804-mj-nicholls?shelf=central-europe">central-europe</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2386804-mj-nicholls?shelf=short-stories">short-stories</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2936956235" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3909964181477555944">A peerless collection of stunning fables, bursting with imaginative madness and the most impressive punnilinguistics in sci-fi. The inclusion of this collection in Penguin Classics proves that Lem is one of the most original writers of the last century, not merely under the sci-fi bumbershoot, in the whole of bigbookdom. Also a feat of remarkable translation from Michael Kandel, on a par with the Oulipian masters Gilbert Adair or Barbara Wright.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3017424068" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3017424068" />
    <a title="George" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/21007191-george"><img alt="George" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1542795267p2/21007191.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3017424068?book_show_action=true">Oct 30, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="George" class="user" itemprop="url" name="George" href="/user/show/21007191-george">George</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780141394596" href="/book/show/21079821-the-cyberiad">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3017424068" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9035054816373461770"><i>The Cyberiad</i> is truly a cybernetic Iliad, forged from chromium chronicles, cobalt fables, metallic mythologies, titanium tales, stainless steel stories, platinum parables, magnesium sagas, wrought iron records, copper epics, silicon sagas, lead legends, all-around space-age aluminum alloygories.<br/><br/>This is one of the best story collections I have ever read period (comparable in quality to Borge's <i>Ficciones</i>). It's a story cycle, mostly revolving around the strange adventures and mechanical mishaps of</span>
  <span id="freeText9035054816373461770" style="display:none"><i>The Cyberiad</i> is truly a cybernetic Iliad, forged from chromium chronicles, cobalt fables, metallic mythologies, titanium tales, stainless steel stories, platinum parables, magnesium sagas, wrought iron records, copper epics, silicon sagas, lead legends, all-around space-age aluminum alloygories.<br /><br />This is one of the best story collections I have ever read period (comparable in quality to Borge's <i>Ficciones</i>). It's a story cycle, mostly revolving around the strange adventures and mechanical mishaps of two robotic intellectricians, Trurl and Klapaucius, so it's not as disparate as other collections tend to be. This book also serves as an example of pristine and galvanic translation. I have no idea what the original Polish reads like but Lem's singular imagination is brought to alien life with interplanetary puns, cosmic portmanteaus, and otherworldly descriptions. Indubitably evocative, humorous, and clever.<br /><br />I started reading for enjoyment in middle school and would often read anything with a dragon in it. As it happens, there is a highly intellectual and witty story about dragons. But every story is packed with heart and mind, a weird and delightful fusion of old and new loves. Storytelling at its finest and most exuberant. A delight from start to finish. In a word (or two), passionate virtuosity.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9035054816373461770" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer3556298005331387379">Another masterwork of this brilliant writter.<br/><br/>Obviously i have read this work in spanish because this polish collection of tales is almost intranslatable,it is full of fun neologisms of all sort.<br/>It is a extremely funny and satiric book,but also serious deep in almost all branches of philosophy,transhumanism and physics .<br/>Lem builds a astounding medieval, cibernetic,mechanic world were he develops the adventures of two ciberetic beings ,the builders,Trul and Claupacius.<br/>Below this apparently absurd </span>
  <span id="freeText3556298005331387379" style="display:none">Another masterwork of this brilliant writter.<br /><br />Obviously i have read this work in spanish because this polish collection of tales is almost intranslatable,it is full of fun neologisms of all sort.<br />It is a extremely funny and satiric book,but also serious deep in almost all branches of philosophy,transhumanism and physics .<br />Lem builds a astounding medieval, cibernetic,mechanic world were he develops the adventures of two ciberetic beings ,the builders,Trul and Claupacius.<br />Below this apparently absurd and grotesque fables,full of distorted philosophic ,matematical and physical neologisms ,underlie many times deep concepts of philosophy and advanced phisics and mathematics,carried to bizarre limits.<br />For put a example,we have the astounding tale "The Dragons of Probability" where the builders make a machine that increases the probability till near 1,as a consecuence very improbable events become real and the second law of termodinamics is broken,this lead to very improbable arranges of the matter that makes possible the existence of dragons,the spontaneus motion of inert stones and so on.Other long tale touch subjects as the existence of God or themans creating robots and robots creating mans in a infinite loop.<br /><br />A joker,satiric,extremely original and deep work that one reads with a big smile from the begining till the end.<br />Without doubt reccomendable .<br /></span>

<div id="review_249396033" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/249396033" />
    <a title="Voss Foster" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7221225-voss-foster"><img alt="Voss Foster" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442642523p2/7221225.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/249396033?book_show_action=true">Dec 20, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Voss Foster" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Voss Foster" href="/user/show/7221225-voss-foster">Voss Foster</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7221225-voss-foster?shelf=absolute-must-reads">absolute-must-reads</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7221225-voss-foster?shelf=science-fiction">science-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7221225-voss-foster?shelf=book-club-reads">book-club-reads</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7221225-voss-foster?shelf=rainy-day-books">rainy-day-books</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7221225-voss-foster?shelf=sure-to-be-classics">sure-to-be-classics</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer249396033" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer498168651620927444">I first ran across The Cyberiad in desperation. It takes me next to no time to read books, so I quickly drained every last inch of our bookshelves by eighth grade, and the library had nothing.<br/><br/>My dear lord. Before I get into the writing itself, let's not forget the briliant translation, and this book would not be easy to translate, between alliterations, rhyming, and the sheerly brilliant nonsense (I use brilliant so much because one simply can't use that word enough when speaking of this book.),</span>
  <span id="freeText498168651620927444" style="display:none">I first ran across The Cyberiad in desperation. It takes me next to no time to read books, so I quickly drained every last inch of our bookshelves by eighth grade, and the library had nothing.<br /><br />My dear lord. Before I get into the writing itself, let's not forget the briliant translation, and this book would not be easy to translate, between alliterations, rhyming, and the sheerly brilliant nonsense (I use brilliant so much because one simply can't use that word enough when speaking of this book.), he had a Herculean challenge and met it.<br /><br />Now, when it comes to the writing of Lem himself, you reach a new level. Most science-fiction fans will gladly preach the gospel of the European sci-fi authors, and Lem is unquestionably a great among them, in no small part due to The Cyberiad. While fans of hard sci-fi may find the ridiculous, operatic, humorous, pseudo-scientific stories pretty much revolting (though I doubt it), no one can deny the uniqueness of his worldbuilding (surprisingly deep, for nonsense) or the fabulous anti-hero, Trurl.<br /><br />I feel terrible not being able to give more, in case someone hasn't read it, but it would not be right to reveal the glory here. Suffice it to say that this should live on every bookshelf, genre-bound or literati, and should be reread often and with much gusto. A true piece of art.</span>
  <a data-text-id="498168651620927444" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_155955630" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/155955630" />
    <a title="Nate D" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/406701-nate-d"><img alt="Nate D" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1203897989p2/406701.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/155955630?book_show_action=true">Mar 22, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Nate D" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Nate D" href="/user/show/406701-nate-d">Nate D</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780156235501" href="/book/show/1118485.The_Cyberiad">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">gnostotron technicians</span></div>

          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Nate D by: </span>
      turboservoserfs
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/406701-nate-d?shelf=read-in-2011">read-in-2011</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/406701-nate-d?shelf=poland">poland</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/406701-nate-d?shelf=warsaw-pact-bureau-of-the-arts">warsaw-pact-bureau-of-the-arts</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/406701-nate-d?shelf=sci-fi">sci-fi</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer155955630" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6334655532780784789">Cybernetic fables, simultaneously very old and very new. At his best, Lem is playful and wise in the manner of certain Calvino. At his worst, he tales off into long strings of silly words and technobabble puns. As such, I had to take a few breaks, but ended up being well rewarded for my time: the later stories-within-stories-within-stories (a nested Arabian Nights, or rather a <i>Sarragossa Manuscript</i>) seem to really be making an attempt to interrogate the universe, and its observations are sad and</span>
  <span id="freeText6334655532780784789" style="display:none">Cybernetic fables, simultaneously very old and very new. At his best, Lem is playful and wise in the manner of certain Calvino. At his worst, he tales off into long strings of silly words and technobabble puns. As such, I had to take a few breaks, but ended up being well rewarded for my time: the later stories-within-stories-within-stories (a nested Arabian Nights, or rather a <i>Sarragossa Manuscript</i>) seem to really be making an attempt to interrogate the universe, and its observations are sad and thoughtful beneath the clowning. I even actually felt myself drawn a little closer to the characters, props and automata that they may well be. Really, that's the main issue I had here: these really are fables more than stories, and as such it's difficult to really get close to the characters or narratives for the most part. But fables have there place of course, and many of these are good ones.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6334655532780784789" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer11421231938421918710">Originally I was just thrilled to find a SF book by an author actually in Poland. But, after I read the book, I was amazed. Still one of the funniest books I have ever read. Two competing robots (Trurl and Klaupacius) who try to out-invent each other, create some of the most wild constructs that anyone could ever imagine.<br/><br/>One being the machine Trurl creates that can make anthing that starts with the letter 'N'. Things really get wild when Klaupacius tests the machine by asking it to create 'nothi</span>
  <span id="freeText11421231938421918710" style="display:none">Originally I was just thrilled to find a SF book by an author actually in Poland. But, after I read the book, I was amazed. Still one of the funniest books I have ever read. Two competing robots (Trurl and Klaupacius) who try to out-invent each other, create some of the most wild constructs that anyone could ever imagine.<br /><br />One being the machine Trurl creates that can make anthing that starts with the letter 'N'. Things really get wild when Klaupacius tests the machine by asking it to create 'nothing'.<br /><br />But my favorite was the three story tall calculating machine. But no matter how many times the massive computer is reprogrammed, whenever asked the answer to the equation of '2 + 2' is always '7'! And the machine is quite definative about it's answer too. I like these passages so much I used them in my college speech class.<br /><br />If you could use a good laugh, 'The Cyberaid' is the answer! </span>

<div id="review_7919391" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/7919391" />
    <a title="Greg" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/262254-greg"><img alt="Greg" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1273956732p2/262254.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/7919391?book_show_action=true">Oct 18, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Greg" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Greg" href="/user/show/262254-greg">Greg</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/262254-greg?shelf=speculative-fiction">speculative-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer7919391" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15802637388089822275">I first came across Stanislaw Lem by way of an absolutely fantastic book called <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/181242.The_Mind_s_I_Fantasies_and_Reflections_on_Self___Soul' title='The Mind's I Fantasies and Reflections on Self & Soul by Douglas R. Hofstadter' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Mind's I: Fantasies and Reflections on Self and Soul</a> in which three of his short stories were featured. His stories touched on issues in philosophy, topics having to do with artificial intelligence, consciousness, physics, mathematics programming, and more. Upon reading these my thoughts were something along the lines of, "this is one of the most fabulous authors I've ever come across, how have I never heard of h</span>
  <span id="freeText15802637388089822275" style="display:none">I first came across Stanislaw Lem by way of an absolutely fantastic book called <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/181242.The_Mind_s_I_Fantasies_and_Reflections_on_Self___Soul" title="The Mind's I Fantasies and Reflections on Self &amp; Soul by Douglas R. Hofstadter" rel="nofollow noopener">The Mind's I: Fantasies and Reflections on Self and Soul</a> in which three of his short stories were featured. His stories touched on issues in philosophy, topics having to do with artificial intelligence, consciousness, physics, mathematics programming, and more. Upon reading these my thoughts were something along the lines of, "this is one of the most fabulous authors I've ever come across, how have I never heard of him?" I picked up The Cyberiad because I knew it contained two of the short stories I had read and assumed the quality of the book as a whole was on par with my experience, but halfway through The Cyberiad I my thinking was more along the lines of, "what the hell is this?"<br /><br />The Cyberiad is a collection of short stories that take place in a very distant future, revolving around two characters name Trurl and Klaupacius, synthetic conscious robot type lifeforms in a universe where humans are but a distant memory. Trurl and Klaupacius are constructors, which means their profession involves building everything from the smartest machine in the universe, to an entire universe housed within a glass ball. Stanislaw Lem was obviously brilliant. The man understood physics and computing at a level far beyond his time, especially for a science fiction writer. His stories are infused with so much scientific terminology and mathematics that a reader not familiar with these terms might as well be reading gibberish. And herein lies half the problem. While his knowledge of these ideas is obvious, the way he uses them is often times nonsensical. Not only is the use of these terms and ideas mostly nonsensical, but the stories they are contained within and wrapped around are mostly nonsensical themselves. Many of the stories are absurdest. I would assume they were written for children if it wasn't for the complexity of the vocabulary and ideas. <br /><br />For those readers that have read The Neverending Story, picture the story telling style contained within that book(the randomness and seeming inexhaustible supply of non sequiturs and incidental information) in a science fiction setting written by Douglas Adams on acid. It wasn't until over half way through the book before the stories started incorporating a more serious philosophical underbelly. In the end the last few stories were recognizable to me as the author that initially amazed me so. Even till the very end they all contain some element of absurdism, but you can tell there is more going on. If the earlier stories had anything more to them, I must be too dense to have figured it out. This is a bit unfair. Almost every single story within the book had some very smart and ingenious aspect to it. But was for the most part lost within absurd narrative going on around it.<br /><br />Douglas Adams has a way of being absurd that still remains engaging and entertaining and somehow always feels like there is deep meaning infused in each progressive paragraph. Stanislaw Lem had the opportunity to create that, but chose a different path. Still, these stories are FUNNY. And the word play and narrative structure is really brilliant. I was continuously amazed that this wasn't written in english because so much of the comedy and style depended on a very specific structure. Kudos to whoever did this translation.<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="15802637388089822275" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_2847574498" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2847574498" />
    <a title="Jamie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/53587416-jamie"><img alt="Jamie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1571965612p2/53587416.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2847574498?book_show_action=true">Jun 06, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jamie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jamie" href="/user/show/53587416-jamie">Jamie</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2847574498" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5302302131771681834">Very amusing fable like stories about two friends and rivals, with nearly god-like skill as inventors and builders of machines. They travel the galaxy, seeking to utilize their skills to solve various problems, typically on behalf of a rich prince or king. The machines usually turn out not quite as expected. Like Henry Kuttner's classic <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/19003281.The_Proud_Robot' title='The Proud Robot by Henry Kuttner' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Proud Robot</a>.<br/><br/>There's really nothing quite like <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/10991.Stanis_aw_Lem' title='Stanisław Lem' rel='nofollow noopener'>Stanisław Lem</a>, wildly imaginative and wholly original. Like Kuttner and <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/8489.Robert_Sheckley' title='Robert Sheckley' rel='nofollow noopener'>Robert Sheckley</a>, much of his writings h</span>
  <span id="freeText5302302131771681834" style="display:none">Very amusing fable like stories about two friends and rivals, with nearly god-like skill as inventors and builders of machines. They travel the galaxy, seeking to utilize their skills to solve various problems, typically on behalf of a rich prince or king. The machines usually turn out not quite as expected. Like Henry Kuttner's classic <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/19003281.The_Proud_Robot" title="The Proud Robot by Henry Kuttner" rel="nofollow noopener">The Proud Robot</a>.<br /><br />There's really nothing quite like <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/10991.Stanis_aw_Lem" title="Stanisław Lem" rel="nofollow noopener">Stanisław Lem</a>, wildly imaginative and wholly original. Like Kuttner and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/8489.Robert_Sheckley" title="Robert Sheckley" rel="nofollow noopener">Robert Sheckley</a>, much of his writings have a witty, humorous edge. I don't know if there's a deeper message in these stories, about the inherent fallibility of machines and robots, or if they are intended purely as entertainment. Maybe a bit of both. Quite a bit of the humor seems rooted in mathematics, though Lem himself was not a mathematician, scientist or engineer as far as I know.<br /><br />There's something about these stories that make me think of Jack Vance's classic <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/951749.The_Dying_Earth__The_Dying_Earth___1_" title="The Dying Earth (The Dying Earth, #1) by Jack Vance" rel="nofollow noopener">The Dying Earth</a> series. I could easily imagine the robots as two rival wizards doing their best to outdo each other, or some despotic monarch, with absurd demonstrations of their skill. The stories are generally very short, and while all are entertaining I think they're best enjoyed in small doses as others have noted.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5302302131771681834" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_553944076" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/553944076" />
    <a title="Stephen Banks" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/16797016-stephen-banks"><img alt="Stephen Banks" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442658940p2/16797016.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/553944076?book_show_action=true">Mar 06, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Stephen Banks" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Stephen Banks" href="/user/show/16797016-stephen-banks">Stephen Banks</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer553944076" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7432427871489126904">Short form SciFi at it's best. <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/search/search?q=Stanislaw%20Lem' title='Stanislaw Lem' rel='nofollow noopener'>Stanislaw Lem</a> departs from his occasionally dour disposition (see: <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/95558.Solaris' title='Solaris by Stanisław Lem' rel='nofollow noopener'>Solaris</a>) with a series of very funny but also deeply philosophical "journeys" of a pair of Cybernetic engineers (Trurl and Klapacius). Each journey is a short story that stands alone, yet the whole collection is a complete consistent work. Lem uses absurdist plots and situations to poke fun at politics, religion, romance, war and even science. <br/><br/>The translation into English is phenomenal, keeping an i</span>
  <span id="freeText7432427871489126904" style="display:none">Short form SciFi at it's best. <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/search/search?q=Stanislaw%20Lem" title="Stanislaw Lem" rel="nofollow noopener">Stanislaw Lem</a> departs from his occasionally dour disposition (see: <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/95558.Solaris" title="Solaris by Stanisław Lem" rel="nofollow noopener">Solaris</a>) with a series of very funny but also deeply philosophical "journeys" of a pair of Cybernetic engineers (Trurl and Klapacius). Each journey is a short story that stands alone, yet the whole collection is a complete consistent work. Lem uses absurdist plots and situations to poke fun at politics, religion, romance, war and even science. <br /><br />The translation into English is phenomenal, keeping an incredible amount of linguistic play and puns. In one of the stories, Klapacius challenges Trurl to construct a machine that can create anything that begins with the letter N. Trurl succeeds, though the machine can't make Natrium (plain old salt) because, if it could make anything that began with N in any language in the universe, it could be a machine that could make anything at all, which is clearly impossible. Klapacius then orders the machine to make Nothing, and the valiant constructors must act quickly to save the universe. In another, a poetry machine creates an epic love poem all in the language of tensor algebra.<br /><br />It's hard to over-recommend this wonderful work. I must have read it at least a couple dozen times.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7432427871489126904" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2542433049" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2542433049" />
    <a title="Josh" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5366206-josh"><img alt="Josh" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1445467353p2/5366206.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2542433049?book_show_action=true">Nov 11, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Josh" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Josh" href="/user/show/5366206-josh">Josh</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2542433049" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3803803560341266096">Other reviews have acknowledged two critical points I’d like to reinforce. <br/><br/>1. This translation is fantastic. Lem makes his money off word play if The Cyberiad is any indicator, and how in the hell someone was able to work that in a polish to English translation is amazing.<br/><br/>2. These stories might be best consumed separately, rather than on the run.<br/><br/>This is at times a funny book. The story about the machine that makes poetry has a nice satirical spice. The femfatalatron and King Balereon were both a</span>
  <span id="freeText3803803560341266096" style="display:none">Other reviews have acknowledged two critical points I’d like to reinforce. <br /><br />1. This translation is fantastic. Lem makes his money off word play if The Cyberiad is any indicator, and how in the hell someone was able to work that in a polish to English translation is amazing.<br /><br />2. These stories might be best consumed separately, rather than on the run.<br /><br />This is at times a funny book. The story about the machine that makes poetry has a nice satirical spice. The femfatalatron and King Balereon were both absurdist and silly. Amongst all the sallies are loads of invented words and puns that will give a giggle if you’re in the know. Some of the references are related to mathematical or engineering principles that may exceed the layman, such as myself, but might be a hoot otherwise. However, even if you don’t get the humor, the allegorical nature of the stories usually delivers a payoff. I found that the word play and exposition of each story usually dragged a bit, but once the conflict was established, the stories were very engaging, the lessons thought provoking, if perhaps a bit dated. <br /><br />The Cyberiad is a nice little rip. It gets a clean 4 stars.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3803803560341266096" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_1075964968" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1075964968" />
    <a title="Melanti" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3034846-melanti"><img alt="Melanti" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1286146966p2/3034846.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1075964968?book_show_action=true">Oct 09, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Melanti" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Melanti" href="/user/show/3034846-melanti">Melanti</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/18941848-the-cyberiad">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3034846-melanti?shelf=e-book">e-book</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3034846-melanti?shelf=ku-borrowed">ku-borrowed</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3034846-melanti?shelf=sci-fi">sci-fi</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3034846-melanti?shelf=2014">2014</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3034846-melanti?shelf=anthology">anthology</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1075964968" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9510803873542602806">If I had to pick just one word to describe Lem's fiction, it would be "experimental." All the books by him that I've read so far have been so incredibly different from one another - and often different from anything else I've read as well!<br/><br/>This particular book is a book of short stories about a pair of robots who run around the universe constructing other robots. In many places, it really reminds me of folklore trickster tales, in other places The Arabian Nights Entertainments, and in still other</span>
  <span id="freeText9510803873542602806" style="display:none">If I had to pick just one word to describe Lem's fiction, it would be "experimental." All the books by him that I've read so far have been so incredibly different from one another - and often different from anything else I've read as well!<br /><br />This particular book is a book of short stories about a pair of robots who run around the universe constructing other robots. In many places, it really reminds me of folklore trickster tales, in other places The Arabian Nights Entertainments, and in still others just common silliness. It's a lot of fun and a whole lot more accessible than some of his other work (such as <i>Memoirs Found in a Bathtub</i> that inspired the "lemming" of Sword &amp; Laser infamy) and I think it would appeal to even those who aren't die-hard sci-fi fans.<br /><br />In addition, I've got to say this is one of the most phenomenal pieces of translation I've come across in a long, long time. All the fantastic jokes and wordplay - including made up words, alliteration, poetry, word games - are all translated wonderfully. They're not word-for-word translations, of course. Such a thing wouldn't work at all with this sort of book. But Kandel took enough liberties with the text that if a joke just couldn't be directly translated and still be funny, he replaced it with a similar reading English joke. Which is rather daring, but that's really the only way this sort of book could ever be translated.<br /><br />There's more than a few OCR errors through out but it's not NEARLY as bad as the other Stanislaw Lem books I've bought so I guess they at least ran this particular volume through spell check. Whew!</span>
  <a data-text-id="9510803873542602806" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <a title="Gabriella" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7522270-gabriella"><img alt="Gabriella" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1327454208p2/7522270.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/613213022?book_show_action=true">May 11, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Gabriella" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Gabriella" href="/user/show/7522270-gabriella">Gabriella</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7522270-gabriella?shelf=disappointing">disappointing</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7522270-gabriella?shelf=philosophy">philosophy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7522270-gabriella?shelf=why-did-i-read-this">why-did-i-read-this</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7522270-gabriella?shelf=sci-fi">sci-fi</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7522270-gabriella?shelf=character-heavy">character-heavy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7522270-gabriella?shelf=futuristic">futuristic</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer613213022" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8895636338916369185"><strong>Stories:</strong> A mix of good, boring, thought-provoking, and bad.<br/><strong>Main characters:</strong> Seldom comedic. A bit whiney. Emotionally uninvesting.<br/><strong>Writing:</strong> Okay at first, but very annoying by the end. I lost interest after the writing structure started to become more and more ridiculous and hard to read/understand. By trying to be creative, it basically seemed like he used a math or science term and added a couple letters to the end of it to make it sound latin. It felt very forced and unimaginative. A couple of</span>
  <span id="freeText8895636338916369185" style="display:none"><strong>Stories:</strong> A mix of good, boring, thought-provoking, and bad.<br /><strong>Main characters:</strong> Seldom comedic. A bit whiney. Emotionally uninvesting.<br /><strong>Writing:</strong> Okay at first, but very annoying by the end. I lost interest after the writing structure started to become more and more ridiculous and hard to read/understand. By trying to be creative, it basically seemed like he used a math or science term and added a couple letters to the end of it to make it sound latin. It felt very forced and unimaginative. A couple of these in a paragraph to give something a name would have been fine, but every sentence seemed almost made up of this gibberish (Thisicus wouldabar be the naminad of a personia or a sentencera). In addition to replacing the simplest of words with long strung-out technical sounding jargon, some of the character names seemed to lack creativity as well (a character named Mygrayne - aka migraine?). The chapters that did not have this sort of writing were far more bearable to read and enjoyable.<br /><strong>Summary:</strong> All in all, this was a giant disappointment. It seemed interesting enough at first, but the farther and farther I got into the book, the less I wanted to read it. I'm not quite sure what people see in this book. Maybe it was better in the original Polish version and got lost somewhere in the translation.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8895636338916369185" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer8024665189652734175">An interesting and sometimes surprising collection of science fiction fairytales, each with its own moral still relevant today. I can't imagine what creativity and skill the translator must have possessed to work with all the newly created words, names and even rhymes (some stories almost could be read like poetry or something).   <span id="freeText8024665189652734175" style="display:none">An interesting and sometimes surprising collection of science fiction fairytales, each with its own moral still relevant today. I can't imagine what creativity and skill the translator must have possessed to work with all the newly created words, names and even rhymes (some stories almost could be read like poetry or something). Some stories I enjoyed more, some less, but I was generally rooting for our constructors Trurl and Klapaucius all the way. In the Cyberiad, the universe is much bigger and much more surprising than one could think but robot or human (excuse me, paleface) - we all seem to deal with the same challenges.</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer6202986188939983315">I have to give this book an award for Best Chapter Title:<br/><br/>"The Fourth Sally, or How Trurl Built a Femfatalatron to Save Prince Pantagoon from the Pangs of Love, and How Later He Resorted to a Cannonade of Babies."<br/><br/>While the chapter on dragons is by far my favorite sally, mostly for the beginning theoretical explanations of how dragons cannot exist, except by bizarre partial probability equations.   <span id="freeText6202986188939983315" style="display:none">I have to give this book an award for Best Chapter Title:<br /><br />"The Fourth Sally, or How Trurl Built a Femfatalatron to Save Prince Pantagoon from the Pangs of Love, and How Later He Resorted to a Cannonade of Babies."<br /><br />While the chapter on dragons is by far my favorite sally, mostly for the beginning theoretical explanations of how dragons cannot exist, except by bizarre partial probability equations. Ingenious.<br /><br />As for Sally 1A, isn't it a bit bizarre that a robot builds the ultimate poetry machine, and that all the other robot poets are shamed by it? Considering it says, rather specifically, that machines cannot write verse (in that they have no soul, I believe it was--this was rather a lot of sallies ago)? Which is an interesting discussion point on the difference between robot and machine.<br /><br />Overall, a masterpiece of science, mathematics, imagination, philosophy, theology, satire, robotkind, and humankind. An epic of the future, if Odysseus were a robot.</span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1774732978" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8650956690377631538">Overall, very funny. Though, as some stories did lag a bit, I was inclined to give this book a 3.5, maybe even a 3.7 . BUT, and this is a very big but, the translation is AMAZING and earns the book a whole star on its own. Lem is a great author but Michael Kandel is a genius. Math and philosophy jokes... restrictive poetry... restrictive poetic math joke - translated fluidly into English. I call shenanigans the only explanation is shenanigans. <br/><br/>But on to the text. Cyberiad the hilarious tales of </span>
  <span id="freeText8650956690377631538" style="display:none">Overall, very funny. Though, as some stories did lag a bit, I was inclined to give this book a 3.5, maybe even a 3.7 . BUT, and this is a very big but, the translation is AMAZING and earns the book a whole star on its own. Lem is a great author but Michael Kandel is a genius. Math and philosophy jokes... restrictive poetry... restrictive poetic math joke - translated fluidly into English. I call shenanigans the only explanation is shenanigans. <br /><br />But on to the text. Cyberiad the hilarious tales of two "constructors" who travel the universe and get into trouble or glory. Not explicitly stated until later in the book but every character is a robot<a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[, save a single (human?) on the second last page <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>. Mechanic life evolved from robot ancestors who broke free of their chains from the earlier, "squishy" water based beings that created them. Confirmation later that this is in *our* universe (reference to the Crab Nebula), but as to when, I can't exactly be so sure.<br />Laughs abound, though as I said earlier a couple of the stories did run long, making it hard to get through. I like seeing "Sally" as a noun (from the verb eg 'sally forth'), I don't know if I've seen that before. Illustrations were great.<br /><br />Finally, though I'm sure other posters have done it, I need to share the math poem for you all. Genius in design and genius in translation. A poetry machine is asked to provide "A love poem, lyrical, pastoral, and expressed in the language of pure mathematics."<br /><a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[
  <br />
  <blockquote>Come, let us hasten to a higher plane,<br />Where dryads tread the very fields of Venn<br />Their indices bedecked from one to <i>n</i><br />Commingled in an endless Markov chain.<br /><br />Come, every frustum longs to be a cone,<br />And every vector dreams of matrices.<br />Hark to the gentle gradient of the breeze:<br />It whispers of a more ergodic zone<br /><br />In Riemann, Hilbert, or in Banach space<br />Let superscripts and subscripts go their ways<br />Our asymptotes no longer out of phase,<br />We shall encounter, counting, face to face.<br /><br />I'll grant thee random access to my heart,<br />Thou'lt tell me all the constants of thy love;<br />And two shall love's lemmas prove<br />And in our bound partition never part,<br /><br />For what did Cauchy know, or Christoffel,,<br />Or Fournier, or any Boole or Euler,<br />Wielding their compasses, their pens and rulers,<br />Of thy supernal sinusoidal spell?<br /><br />Cancel me not - for what then shall remain?<br />Abscissas, some mantissas, modules, modes,<br />A root or two, a torus and a node:<br />The inverse of my verse, a null domain.<br /><br />Ellipse of bliss, converse, O lips divine!<br />The product of our scalars is defined!<br /><i>Cyberiad</i> draws nigh, and the skew mind<br />Cuts capers like a happy haversine.<br /><br />I see the eigenvalue in thine eye,<br />I hear the tender tensor in thy sigh.<br />Bernoulli would have been content to die,<br />Had he but known such <i>a² cos 2φ!</i></blockquote>
 <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br />(original emphasis)</span>

<div id="review_321437122" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/321437122" />
    <a title="Sandy Parsons" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3042473-sandy-parsons"><img alt="Sandy Parsons" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1260810531p2/3042473.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/321437122?book_show_action=true">Apr 30, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sandy Parsons" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sandy Parsons" href="/user/show/3042473-sandy-parsons">Sandy Parsons</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3042473-sandy-parsons?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3042473-sandy-parsons?shelf=science-fiction">science-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3042473-sandy-parsons?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer321437122" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9625559724302386006">Have you ever wanted to hug a book and kiss its cover, reserve a special place on your bookshelf so you can look forward to reading it again? This is a unique book. It's funny and smart philosophical science fiction, which isn't for everyone, but if you fall into that demographic, it's the archetype. I've had it on my 'to read' list for a long time, but bumped it up after listening to a podcast interview with David X. Cohen of Futurama. He didn't say explicitly it was an inspiration, but I notic</span>
  <span id="freeText9625559724302386006" style="display:none">Have you ever wanted to hug a book and kiss its cover, reserve a special place on your bookshelf so you can look forward to reading it again? This is a unique book. It's funny and smart philosophical science fiction, which isn't for everyone, but if you fall into that demographic, it's the archetype. I've had it on my 'to read' list for a long time, but bumped it up after listening to a podcast interview with David X. Cohen of Futurama. He didn't say explicitly it was an inspiration, but I noticed a certain flavor to the stories reminiscent of the show. It even has a character named Calculon. I couldn't help but picture the HPLDs as Nibbler's race, the bureauocrat as Hermes, Trurl as Prof Farnsworth, several of the constructs as Bender, etc. It's a rare thing for something to be fun and thought-provoking at the same time. Easy, fantastic read, all the more impressive since it was originally written in Polish.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9625559724302386006" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <a title="Spacewanderer" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6966715-spacewanderer"><img alt="Spacewanderer" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1330986759p2/6966715.jpg" /></a>

        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/439989320?book_show_action=true">Oct 22, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Spacewanderer" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Spacewanderer" href="/user/show/6966715-spacewanderer">Spacewanderer</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780156235501" href="/book/show/1118485.The_Cyberiad">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer439989320" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9356930565911956222">I want to start off by saying that I rarely enjoy reading short fiction. I find it hard to commit or give a damn and I just want to get it all over with...like most people would feel about a common household chore. So many are nothing more than whispers of plots involving under-developed, overly-melodramatic characters that will be left behind after 20 pages, so why bother. Others seem to serve as nothing more than an author's literary masturbation (trademarked, not to be used without written pe</span>
  <span id="freeText9356930565911956222" style="display:none">I want to start off by saying that I rarely enjoy reading short fiction. I find it hard to commit or give a damn and I just want to get it all over with...like most people would feel about a common household chore. So many are nothing more than whispers of plots involving under-developed, overly-melodramatic characters that will be left behind after 20 pages, so why bother. Others seem to serve as nothing more than an author's literary masturbation (trademarked, not to be used without written permission of Spacewanderer, Inc., 2012), snapshots of the human condition framed in tiny little shadowboxes that, hopefully, were more rewarding for the author to write than they are for me to read. The fact of the matter is, the short story is fundamentally flawed and, as such, extremely difficult to write well. Not that there aren't good short stories and talented short story writers out there. It's just that there are so few. And even fewer entire collections of stories.<br /><br />I found "The Cyberiad" to be one of those rare examples. I actually cared about the two main characters, partly because they are likable, and partly because they are resident in each of the stories, so you get to know them. But, more importantly, stuff actually happens. Stories have conflict AND resolution. What a concept! As far as I'm concerned, this book should be required reading in every MFA writer's program in the United States.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9356930565911956222" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_113125956" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/113125956" />
    <a title="David" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/801248-david"><img alt="David" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1554368781p2/801248.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/113125956?book_show_action=true">Jul 23, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="David" class="user" itemprop="url" name="David" href="/user/show/801248-david">David</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/801248-david?shelf=insanely-brilliant-sci-fi">insanely-brilliant-sci-fi</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer113125956" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13242459286135825750">This book has the best first line of any book in world literature ("one day Trurl the constructor put together a machine that could create anything starting with the letter n.") It's definitely my favorite Lem, too. By far the funniest. Some of the Pirx the Pilot stories are almost as funny, but never with such consistency.<br/><br/>Probably can't add too much to the other reviews except to say: I actually read this before I read Rabelais, so I didn't realize how much this is a modern version of the same </span>
  <span id="freeText13242459286135825750" style="display:none">This book has the best first line of any book in world literature ("one day Trurl the constructor put together a machine that could create anything starting with the letter n.") It's definitely my favorite Lem, too. By far the funniest. Some of the Pirx the Pilot stories are almost as funny, but never with such consistency.<br /><br />Probably can't add too much to the other reviews except to say: I actually read this before I read Rabelais, so I didn't realize how much this is a modern version of the same thing - well, minus the crude humor, etc, but totally running with the crazy mock scholarship, over-educated characters making absurd lists and doing utterly inappropriate things. Making all the characters robots makes it... might one even speak of an intellectual carnivalesque? There's a tradition of authors who just purely play with ideas (Borges, Calvino are common comparisons) but no one else I can think of has nearly so much fun with it. </span>
  <a data-text-id="13242459286135825750" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_255470374" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/255470374" />
    <a title="Jason Plein" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4200336-jason-plein"><img alt="Jason Plein" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/m_50x66-82093808bca726cb3249a493fbd3bd0f.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/255470374?book_show_action=true">Jan 03, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jason Plein" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jason Plein" href="/user/show/4200336-jason-plein">Jason Plein</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer255470374" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2820086267554559829">There's a blurb on the back of the book comparing Lem to Borges, which is about right: there is a long section towards the end which is stories nested in stories nested in stories, one of which is a story about someone trapped in a labyrinth of dreams nested in dreams nested in dreams, and there's a story that is sort of a sillier, sci-fi version of Borges' story "The Immortals". What a comparison like that misses is just how silly and playful these stories are.</span>

          </span>
        </div>
