<div id="review_1933351477" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1933351477" />
    <a title="Paul Ataua" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/33831018-paul-ataua"><img alt="Paul Ataua" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1407668489p2/33831018.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1933351477?book_show_action=true">Mar 06, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Paul Ataua" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Paul Ataua" href="/user/show/33831018-paul-ataua">Paul Ataua</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1933351477" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2911220492321334348">I love Lem’s longer works and came to “The Cyberiad” following classics like ‘Solaris’, ‘The Invincible’, and ‘His Master’s Voice’ . Sadly, I was disappointed with this selection. The stories, following two inventors, are at times clever and sometimes vaguely humorous, but neither of those qualities endeared me to them. The creation of a machine that can create anything that starts with the letter N was a high point, but it was pretty much downhill from there on. </span>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_120051952" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/120051952" />
    <a title="Alan Marchant" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2485937-alan-marchant"><img alt="Alan Marchant" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1247361337p2/2485937.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/120051952?book_show_action=true">Sep 04, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Alan Marchant" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Alan Marchant" href="/user/show/2485937-alan-marchant">Alan Marchant</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2485937-alan-marchant?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2485937-alan-marchant?shelf=sci-fi">sci-fi</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2485937-alan-marchant?shelf=comedy">comedy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer120051952" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11739910448595595679">A very lighthearted allegory about the foibles of enlightened human reason as represented by two peripatetic robots, Trurl and Klapaucius. Lem is at his best with the pseudoscientific wordplay. The translator (Michael Kandel) deserves high praise for unobtrusively maintaining the playful mix of references (scientific, philosophical, cultural, sexual, etc) in this English version.</span>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('ZihHK8zoNee0BzoPxKMllsSx0X5yz/kk1bxnLdf+Fmq517AQdl6XKea99iuJcxdF/UQzXJi/4ua+8ATRDJGIkQ==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('BIeAwUZ4cCWA2emuvAFV55x+vJi6uOTuoqOPvA8dZUPbeHf6/M7S69JjJYrx0Wc0pYteulDI/yzJ7+xA1HL7uA==')})
  }

      <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/10991.Stanis_aw_Lem">About Stanisław Lem</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/10991.Stanis_aw_Lem"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1246185166p3/10991.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/10991.Stanis_aw_Lem">Stanisław Lem
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
2,793
followers
</div>

</div>
</div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer10065852931561465629">Stanisław Lem (staˈɲiswaf lɛm) was a Polish science fiction, philosophical and satirical writer of Jewish descent. His books have been translated into 41 languages and have sold over 27 million copies. He is perhaps best known as the author of <i>Solaris</i>, which has twice been made into a feature film. In 1976, <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/12531.Theodore_Sturgeon' title='Theodore Sturgeon' rel='nofollow noopener'>Theodore Sturgeon</a> claimed that Lem was the most widely read science-fiction writer in the w</span>
  <span id="freeText10065852931561465629" style="display:none">Stanisław Lem (staˈɲiswaf lɛm) was a Polish science fiction, philosophical and satirical writer of Jewish descent. His books have been translated into 41 languages and have sold over 27 million copies. He is perhaps best known as the author of <i>Solaris</i>, which has twice been made into a feature film. In 1976, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/12531.Theodore_Sturgeon" title="Theodore Sturgeon" rel="nofollow noopener">Theodore Sturgeon</a> claimed that Lem was the most widely read science-fiction writer in the world.<br /><br />His works explore philosophical themes; speculation on technology, the nature of intelligence, the impossibility of mutual communication and understanding, despair about human limitations and humankind's place in the universe. They are sometimes presented as fiction, but others are in the form of essays or philosophical books. Translations of his works are difficult and multiple translated versions of his works exist.<br /><br />Lem became truly productive after 1956, when the de-Stalinization period led to the "Polish October", when Poland experienced an increase in freedom of speech. Between 1956 and 1968, Lem authored 17 books. His works were widely translated abroad (although mostly in the Eastern Bloc countries). In 1957 he published his first non-fiction, philosophical book, <i>Dialogi</i> (Dialogues), one of his two most famous philosophical texts along with <i>Summa Technologiae</i> (1964). The Summa is notable for being a unique analysis of prospective social, cybernetic, and biological advances. In this work, Lem discusses philosophical implications of technologies that were completely in the realm of science fiction then, but are gaining importance today—like, for instance, virtual reality and nanotechnology. Over the next few decades, he published many books, both science fiction and philosophical/futurological, although from the 1980s onwards he tended to concentrate on philosophical texts and essays.<br /><br />He gained international fame for <i>The Cyberiad</i>, a series of humorous short stories from a mechanical universe ruled by robots, first published in English in 1974. His best-known novels include <i>Solaris</i> (1961), <i>His Master's Voice</i> (Głos pana, 1968), and the late <i>Fiasco</i> (Fiasko, 1987), expressing most strongly his major theme of the futility of mankind's attempts to comprehend the truly alien. Solaris was made into a film in 1972 by Russian director Andrei Tarkovsky and won a Special Jury Prize at the Cannes Film Festival in 1972; in 2002, Steven Soderbergh directed a Hollywood remake starring George Clooney.<br /><br />He was the cousin of poet <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/5565396.Marian_Hemar" title="Marian Hemar" rel="nofollow noopener">Marian Hemar</a>.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10065852931561465629" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

      <div class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/trivia/work/2371216-cyberiada">Trivia About The Cyberiad</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
        <div class="mediumText">
          2 trivia questions

      <div class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/work/quotes/2371216-cyberiada">Quotes from The Cyberiad</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class="stacked" tyle="padding-bottom:15px">
            <span class="readable">&ldquo;Certainly not! I didn't build a machine to solve ridiculous crossword puzzles! That's hack work, not Great Art! Just give it a topic, any topic, as difficult as you like..."<br />Klapaucius thought, and thought some more. Finally he nodded and said:<br />"Very well. Let's have a love poem, lyrical, pastoral, and expressed in the language of pure mathematics. Tensor algebra mainly, with a little topology and higher calculus, if need be. But with feeling, you understand, and in the cybernetic spirit."<br />"Love and tensor algebra?" Have you taken leave of your senses?" Trurl began, but stopped, for his electronic bard was already declaiming:<br /><br />Come, let us hasten to a higher plane,<br />Where dyads tread the fairy fields of Venn,<br />Their indices bedecked from one to n,<br />Commingled in an endless Markov chain!<br /><br />Come, every frustum longs to be a cone,<br />And every vector dreams of matrices.<br />Hark to the gentle gradient of the breeze:<br />It whispers of a more ergodic zone.<br /><br />In Reimann, Hilbert or in Banach space<br />Let superscripts and subscripts go their ways.<br />Our asymptotes no longer out of phase,<br />We shall encounter, counting, face to face.<br /><br />I'll grant thee random access to my heart,<br />Thou'lt tell me all the constants of thy love;<br />And so we two shall all love's lemmas prove,<br />And in bound partition never part.<br /><br />For what did Cauchy know, or Christoffel,<br />Or Fourier, or any Boole or Euler,<br />Wielding their compasses, their pens and rulers,<br />Of thy supernal sinusoidal spell?<br /><br />Cancel me not--for what then shall remain?<br />Abscissas, some mantissas, modules, modes,<br />A root or two, a torus and a node:<br />The inverse of my verse, a null domain.<br /><br />Ellipse of bliss, converge, O lips divine!<br />The product of our scalars is defined!<br />Cyberiad draws nigh, and the skew mind<br />Cuts capers like a happy haversine.<br /><br />I see the eigenvalue in thine eye,<br />I hear the tender tensor in thy sigh.<br />Bernoulli would have been content to die,<br />Had he but known such a^2 cos 2 phi!&rdquo;</span>
            <nobr>
              &mdash;
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/quotes/1132404-certainly-not-i-didn-t-build-a-machine-to-solve-ridiculous">19 likes</a>
            </nobr>
            <div class="clear"></div>
          </div>
          <div class="stacked" tyle="padding-bottom:15px">
            <span class="readable">&ldquo;...it is easy not to believe in monsters, considerably more difficult to escape their dread and loathsome clutches.&rdquo;</span>
            <nobr>
              &mdash;
